    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b0115b3c449446ea9a61e4ca5dbb2c7e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'db6ee47d-b865-4fe2-b90d-e9c3f935226d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Han/Xiaogang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Cheng/Yonghong" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yonghong Cheng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-name">Yonghong Cheng</p>
                     <p>State Key Lab of Electrical Insulation and Power Equipment, Center of Nanomaterials for Renewable Energy (CNRE), School of Electrical Engineering, Xi'an Jiaotong University, Xi'an, 710049 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Cheng/Yonghong">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d254262421" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) is a promising anode material for sodium–ion batteries (SIBs), which suffer from the intrinsic sluggish ion transferability and poor conductivity. To overcome these drawbacks, a facile strategy is developed to synergistically engineer the lattice defects (i.e., heteroatom doping and oxygen vacancy generation) and the fine microstructure (i.e., carbon hybridization and porous structure) of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode, which efficiently enhances the sodium storage performance. Herein, it is successfully realized that the Si-doping into the MIL-125 metal-organic framework structure, which can be easily converted to SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablets by annealing under inert atmosphere. After NaOH etching SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C which contains unbonded SiO<sub>2</sub> and chemically bonded Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti, thus the lattice Si-doped TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C) nanotablets with rich Ti<sup>3+</sup>/oxygen vacancies and abundant inner pores are developed. When examined as an anode for SIB, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C exhibits a high sodium storage capacity (285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent long-term cycling, and high-rate performances (190&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500 cycles with 95.1% capacity retention). Theoretical calculations indicate that the rich Ti<sup>3+</sup>/oxygen vacancies and Si-doping synergistically contribute to a narrowed bandgap and lower sodiation barrier, which thus lead to fast electron/ion transfer coefficients and the predominant pseudocapacitive sodium storage behavior.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302831-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302831-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The fast/large depletion of fossil fuels and thus resultingsevere environmental pollution have become vital issues that endanger the sustainable development of humankind and our earth, which force people to develop and utilize clean renewable energy (ex., solar, wind, etc.).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0001_R_d254262246e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater. Carbon Chem. Eng. J.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these clean energy is regional and timely, and the related power generation cannot be directly connected to the grid, which requires advanced energy storage technologies. Currently, lithium–ion batteries (LIBs) have become the predominant energy storage devices for decades in the fields of portable electronics, electric vehicles, and smart-grids.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0002_R_d254262246e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Eng. J.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the finite and unevenly distributed lithium source hinders the large-scale application of LIBs owing to their high cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0003_R_d254262246e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Alternatively, with a similar working mechanism and configuration to LIBs, sodium–ion batteries (SIBs) have found the limelight on the basis of low-cost and highly abundant sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0004_R_d254262246e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as a main component, the state-of-the-art commercial anode material for LIBs (namely, graphite) shows poor sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0005_R_d254262246e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the larger Na<sup>+</sup> radius would lead to severe volume variation upon sodiation/desodiation and sluggish reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0006_R_d254262246e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, exploiting novel anode materials with highly efficient sodium storage is still urgent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0007_R_d254262246e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano Chem. Eng. J.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As a promising anode material for SIBs, titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) has shown great potential in the energy storage field because of its non-toxic, low-cost, and environmentally friendly features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0008_R_d254262246e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode can achieve a reversible redox reaction between Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> upon the sodium insertion/extraction, which can deliver a theoretical capacity of 335&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0008_R_d254262246e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0009_R_d254262246e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the lower reaction potential (0.7&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and negligible volume variation of TiO<sub>2</sub> would endow SIBs with high energy density, high safety, as well as long lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0010_R_d254262246e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Eng. J.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the intrinsic poor electric conductivity and the sluggish sodium–ion diffusion would result in unsatisfactory rate performance, thus hindering their practical application toward high-efficiency energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0011_R_d254262246e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To overcome these drawbacks, various strategies for rational structure design have been brought up to enhance the electric conductivity and diffusivity of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0012_R_d254262246e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater. Technol. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, an efficient and commonly used method is to hybridize nanostructured TiO<sub>2</sub> with carbon-based materials (ex., carbon nanotube, graphene, etc.), as the carbon matrices offer a highly conductive electron transfer path and meanwhile prevent the aggregation of nanosized TiO<sub>2</sub>, thereby shortening the ion diffusion path and enhancing the electric conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0013_R_d254262246e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, rational structure engineering (ex., hollow, porous, hierarchical, and low-dimensional structures) would also facilitate the penetration of the electrolyte and provide more active sodium storage sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0014_R_d254262246e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Small">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As another effective solution, defect engineering (ex., oxygen vacancy regulation and heteroatom doping) has also been considered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0015_R_d254262246e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanomicro Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the introduction of oxygen vacancies is able to narrow the energy bandgap and increase the density of states (DOS), thus facilitating the electron/ion conductivity of the TiO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0010_R_d254262246e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Eng. J.">10</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0016_R_d254262246e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The heteroatom doping would induce the charge redistribution, thereby forming an internal electric field, which could enhance the electron transferability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0014_R_d254262246e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Small">14</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0017_R_d254262246e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, introducing alien elements into TiO<sub>2</sub> would also stimulate high chemical reaction activity and reduce the energy barrier of TiO<sub>2</sub>, thereby achieving higher electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0018_R_d254262246e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Chen et al. introduced oxygen vacancies and phosphorus (P) dopants into anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> through reduction and subsequent surface functionalization, thus endowing the TiO<sub>2</sub> with improved reaction kinetics as well as excellent cycle/rate performance (183.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0015_R_d254262246e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanomicro Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, Lu et al. prepared TiO<sub>2</sub> anode with surface P-doping that replaced the Ti<sup>3+</sup> sites by forming P<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds, thus enhancing the surface conductivity and achieving outstanding rate performance (167&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0017_R_d254262246e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Whereas these works only realized the surface doping of TiO<sub>2</sub>, the bulk doping of TiO<sub>2</sub> would lead to higher utilization of the active materials. For example, Yao et&nbsp;al. prepared V/Mo-doped TiO<sub>2</sub>/carbon composite using V/Mo-doped Ti-based metal–organic framework (MIL-125) as the precursor and achieved excellent sodium storage performance (180&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 4500 cycles) by controlling the V/Mo-doping amount.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0019_R_d254262246e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Colloid Interface Sci. Composites, Part B">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even though these strategies have enhanced the sodium storage performance to some extent, the intrinsic sluggish reaction kinetics as well as the poor conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub> are still unsatisfactory and need further improvement.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we demonstrate a facile route to integrate different structural engineering strategies, that is, TiO<sub>2</sub> nanostructuring, carbon hybridization, heteroatom doping, and pore generation, into one pot. In this study, we realized the successful doping of Si species into the MIL-125 nanotablets, serving as precursors, which underwent carbonization and formed SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C with conformal shape. Interestingly, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C contained the unbonded SiO<sub>2</sub> that served as a hard template for pore generation and the chemically bonded Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti as lattice doping. After the removal of SiO<sub>2</sub> by NaOH etching, Si-doped TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C) nanotablets with rich Ti<sup>3+</sup>/oxygen vacancies and abundant macropores were obtained, which exhibited excellent sodium storage performance, including high specific capacity (326&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), long-term cycling stability, high rate capability, and predominantly pseudocapacitive contribution, owing to the enhanced ion/electron transfer rates and reaction kinetics of the novel hybrid structure.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302831-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302831-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1a</a> illustrates the synthetic procedure for Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablets. First, the Si-doped MIL-125 (Si-MIL-125) precursor was prepared through a modified solvothermal method, where tetrapropyl orthosilicate (TPOS) and tetrabutyl titanate (TBT) served as the Si and Ti source, respectively, while the p-phthalic acid (PTA) was used as the organic linkers. For comparison, the pure MIL-125 was also prepared under the same conditions but without the introduction of TPOS, which shows cylindrical tablet-like morphology with apparently angular edges (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information), while the Si-MIL-125 displays a rounded tablet-like shape with similar sizes after Si-doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). The morphology variation is mainly related to competing coordinates between Si<sup>4+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup> and organic ligands, which is commonly seen in other doped MIL-125 materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0019_R_d254262246e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Colloid Interface Sci. Composites, Part B">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns reveal that Si-MIL-125 shows weakened and broader diffraction peaks compared to MIL-125, suggesting that Si participates in coordination between the original metal center and organic ligand (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), and the possible structure of Si-MIL-125 is also provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) for better illustration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0020_R_d254262246e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Eng. J. Appl. Surf. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, the MOF precursor (MIL-125 or Si-MIL-125) was annealed under an inert atmosphere, during which the Ti (or Ti/Si) ions were converted into corresponding oxides, while the PTA linkers were decomposed and carbonized as matrices to encapsulate the TiO<sub>2–x</sub> (or SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>) grains. Note that the carbon would reduce Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>3+</sup> to some extent (as revealed by the electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) analysis). Upon annealing, the MIL-125 converted into TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablets (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a>, Supporting Information), and the HRTEM image reveals two sets of lattice fringes with d spacings of 0.35 and 0.24&nbsp;nm, well corresponding to the (101) and (004) planes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No.21-1272) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/980c0c1d-0582-4b17-83ec-fc885ea69a90/smll202302831-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/980c0c1d-0582-4b17-83ec-fc885ea69a90/smll202302831-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1fba54ea-8b30-41c2-a163-7d3e616daca2/smll202302831-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/980c0c1d-0582-4b17-83ec-fc885ea69a90/smll202302831-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Scheme for the synthesis of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. b) SEM, c) TEM, and d) HRTEM images of SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. e) SEM, f) TEM, and g) HRTEM images of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and h) HAADF-STEM image of a single Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C particle with i) corresponding elemental maps for C/O/Si/Ti elements.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Upon calcinating the Si-MIL-125 in argon, the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablets were obtained, which display conformal morphology but shrunk size, as revealed by the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1b</a>), while the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image clearly reveals the round and porous feature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1c</a>). Differently, as shown in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image, the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C displays ambiguous lattice fringes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1d</a>), which may be related to the introduction of amorphous SiO<sub>2</sub>. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image and corresponding energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) maps were taken on a single SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablet, revealing the even dispersal of C, O, Si, and Ti elements within the particle. After etching the unbonded SiO<sub>2</sub> species in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C by reacting with NaOH solution (SiO<sub>2</sub> (s) + 2NaOH (aq) = Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (aq) + H<sub>2</sub>O (l)), the as obtained Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C still maintains the round tablet morphology, whose surface becomes rougher (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1e</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1f</a>, the TEM image clearly reveals that meso/macropores appear after the etching process, suggesting the successful removal of SiO<sub>2</sub> species. To get more insight into the microstructure of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, HRTEM image was taken at the edge of a tablet (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1g</a>), in which the obvious lattice fringe (0.35&nbsp;nm) can be indexed to (101) plane of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No.21-1272), suggesting that the anatase phase is well preserved after the etching process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1h</a> shows the HAADF-STEM image of a single Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablet, and the EDS images shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0001">1i</a> confirm the presence and the homogeneous distribution of the C/O/Si/Ti. It is noteworthy that the Si element still exists in Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C but with a lower signal. The residual Si is suggested to be chemically bonded with TiO<sub>2</sub>, which was proved by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and will be discussed later.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2a</a> depicts XRD patterns of the products, the pristine TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C show typical diffraction patterns of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, and the diffraction peaks at 25.3, 37.8, and 48.0° correspond to the (101), (004), and (200) planes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No.21-1272).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0021_R_d254262246e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the diffraction peak of SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C shifts to higher angles compared to TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C as seen from the enlarged XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), indicating a smaller d-spacing. This result is in good agreement with the shorter Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond (1.59&nbsp;Å) compared to Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond (1.79&nbsp;Å).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0022_R_d254262246e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Appl. Catal., B Appl. Surf. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After etching with NaOH, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, however, shows much weaker peaks, suggesting that the NaOH etching would affect the TiO<sub>2</sub> crystallization to some extent. The specific surface and porous structure of the as-prepared products were evaluated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2b</a>), and the specific surface areas for the TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C nanotablets are ≈176, 152, and 168&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The similar surface areas are consistent with their similar sizes, and the small difference originates from the fine microstructures. Note that the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C exhibits the lowest specific surface area, which is due to the additional introduction of Si source in the MOF structure, and the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C displays increased specific surface area after NaOH etching, as the SiO<sub>2</sub> serves as a hard template. Furthermore, the pore size and volume features were analyzed through the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which are summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). All the products exhibit a main pore diameter at 3.5&nbsp;nm, and both SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C exhibit larger meso/macropores, which suggest that the introduction of TPOS facilitates the generation of large pores. After NaOH etching, the pore volume for macropores (&gt;100&nbsp;nm) is much higher in Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was conducted to estimate the TiO<sub>2</sub> weight content within the products. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2c</a>, the slight weight drop between 100 and 200&nbsp;°C is ascribed to the removal of absorbed water, while the main weight loss after 300&nbsp;°C is related to the oxidation of carbon, based on which the carbon weight contents in TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C are calculated as 20.0%, 24.1%, and 30.0%, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9d89d7b6-6047-4582-8b76-01eb4dcefe3b/smll202302831-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9d89d7b6-6047-4582-8b76-01eb4dcefe3b/smll202302831-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7593ba73-721d-4ee2-907a-3570f8ea1585/smll202302831-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9d89d7b6-6047-4582-8b76-01eb4dcefe3b/smll202302831-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms with inset showing the pore distribution and c) TGA curves for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. d) Si 2p XPS spectra for SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. e) Ti 2p XPS spectra and f) EPR curves for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>XPS analysis was performed to unravel the chemical state evolution upon Si doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information), in which a new peak at ≈100&nbsp;eV corresponding to Si 2p can be detected in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C in the survey XPS spectra, and then almost disappears in Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C after NaOH etching (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2d</a> compares the high-resolution Si 2p XPS spectra for SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, where the peak at 103.0&nbsp;eV in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C well indexes to Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Si bonds with the highest oxidation state of Si(IV) as SiO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0023_R_d254262246e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Appl. Catal., B">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After NaOH etching, the Si 2p peak shifts toward lower binding energy (101.6&nbsp;eV) with much-weakened intensity, suggesting the generation of Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bond. Specifically, the Si (1.90) exhibits larger electronegativity compared with Ti (1.54), and the formation of Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bond will result in a decrease of positive charge around Si atoms, thereby leading to lowered binding energy in Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0024_R_d254262246e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - RSC Adv. Appl. Surf. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the peak for Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bond cannot be observed in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C due to the strong intensity of Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Si bonds. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2e</a> depicts the Ti 2p spectra for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, where the main peaks at 464.6 and 458.9&nbsp;eV in TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C correspond to the Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2,</sub> respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0025_R_d254262246e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Alloys Compd.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, these peaks shift to lower binding energy after Si doping in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, indicating the existence of Ti<sup>3+</sup> and oxygen vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0019_R_d254262246e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Colloid Interface Sci. Composites, Part B">19</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0026_R_d254262246e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Energy Chem.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Si/(Ti+Si) molar ratio in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C was calculated as 0.396 and 0.158 according to XPS spectra (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the O1s XPS spectra reveal that signals at 530.2 and 532.2&nbsp;eV correspondingly relate to the lattice oxygen and oxygen vacancies in TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0016_R_d254262246e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After Si-doping, the peak at 532.2&nbsp;eV becomes much stronger, suggesting that more oxygen vacancies were formed in SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. Note that Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C exhibits a newly emerged peak at 533.4&nbsp;eV which corresponds to the absorbed water arising from etching treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0015_R_d254262246e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanomicro Lett.">15</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0016_R_d254262246e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, EPR tests were applied to confirm the existence of oxygen vacancies, and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0002">2f</a> reveals an obvious symmetrical peak at <i>g</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.003 in all three products, corresponding to the unpaired electrons caused by oxygen vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0015_R_d254262246e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanomicro Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C shows a weakened EPR signal compared to the pristine TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, possibly due to the generation of SiO<sub>2</sub>. However, after NaOH etching, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C shows a much-enhanced signal, indicating its rich oxygen vacancies, which facilitate enhancing the electrical conductivity and reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0013_R_d254262246e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy Energy Storage Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0014_R_d254262246e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Small">14</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0016_R_d254262246e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage properties of TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C were investigated in half-cells with the potential ranging from 0.01 to 3&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and all the specific capacities and current densities were calculated based on the weight of active materials (TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3a</a> depicts the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode in the first five cycles at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, where two broad peaks located at ≈1.0 and 0.4&nbsp;V can be observed in the initial cathodic sweep, which is related to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, the reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>3+</sup>, and the irreversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0009_R_d254262246e1198" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0027_R_d254262246e1198" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Electrochim. Acta">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following anodic sweep, a weak broad peak at 0.79&nbsp;V corresponds to the oxidation of Ti<sup>3+</sup> to Ti<sup>4+</sup> upon desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0028_R_d254262246e1207" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following cathodic scans, no well-defined but broad reduction peak is observed, suggesting the gradual sodiation of the poor Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub> grains as well as the porous amorphous carbon matrices. Furthermore, both TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C show similar electrochemical reaction mechanisms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), except the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode displays severe polarization in the initial reductive scan due to its poor electron conductivity (which will be discussed in Nyquist plots). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode, which exhibits a high initial discharge/charge capacity of 1099.3/406.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 36.9%, higher than those for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (746.7/211.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with ICE of 28.3%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information) and SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (874.1/253.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with ICE of 29.0%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). The excessive capacity of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C might mainly originate from the Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti heteroatom structure and abundant macropores created by the removal of SiO<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, the introduction of defects (oxygen vacancies, heteroatom doping) will also enhance the reversibility of sodium storage, thus leading to improved ICEs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0029_R_d254262246e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Asian J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the GCD profiles at different current densities are also depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3c</a>, where all the curves show a sloping voltage profile, suggesting a predominated pseudocapacitive behavior, which will be discussed later.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0009_R_d254262246e1266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0dcab968-28cb-4275-b8b3-b694a1619a53/smll202302831-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0dcab968-28cb-4275-b8b3-b694a1619a53/smll202302831-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/98be328a-f9ab-44c9-889c-7de858f464d7/smll202302831-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0dcab968-28cb-4275-b8b3-b694a1619a53/smll202302831-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves, b,c) GCD profiles for Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C under 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and various current densities. d,e) Cycling and rate performances for the TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes. f) Comparison of the rate performance of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C anode with other TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials. g) Cycling performance at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the cycling performance at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which all show slight capacity fading in the initial 20 cycles. The huge irreversible capacity loss is primarily attributed to electrochemical reactions between the surface defects and electrolyte as well as the irreversible reactions of sodium ions in the electrode, which is commonly seen in TiO<sub>2</sub>/carbon-based electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0030_R_d254262246e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the Coulombic efficiency quickly rises above 99%, and the sodium storage capacity becomes stabilized. Interestingly, the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode delivers a higher reversible capacity of 219&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> compared to TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (190&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), which suggests that Si-doping can enhance the sodium storage capacity. After NaOH etching, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode shows much enhanced sodium storage capability, delivering a reversible capacity of 285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles. The improved sodium storage capacity can be attributed to the removal of electrochemically inactive SiO<sub>2</sub> species, the enlarged surface area and macropores, as well as the increased oxygen vacancies, which synergistically facilitate the electrolyte penetration and charge transfer rate. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3e</a> compares the rate performance of the TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes. Apparently, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode exhibits the most excellent rate properties, delivering much higher capacities of 365.4, 329.0, 293.4, 266.4, and 233.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density drops back to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it still retains a much higher reversible capacity of 326.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> than that for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (199.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (239.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3f</a> shows the rate properties of other TiO<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes, which further reveal the excellent rate capability of the current Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0003">3g</a> shows the long-term cycling performance of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, in which the capacity quickly stabilized at 200.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with Coulombic efficiencies over 99% after the initial 20 cycles. Even after 2500 cycles, a reversible capacity of 191.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be maintained with a high-capacity retention of 95.1% (compared to the 20th cycle), suggesting the excellent cycling stability of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode, which overpassed most recently reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The outstanding electrochemical performance of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode can be attributed to the synergetic effect of the heterostructure of Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti as well as oxygen vacancies, which provide extra sodium storage sites and rapid reaction kinetics, and the abundant macropores produced by the removal of SiO<sub>2</sub> facilitate the electrolyte penetration.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the charge/ion transferability, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was measured, and <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the Nyquist plots of the three electrodes, in which the semicircle in high frequency can be used to calculate the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). The origin TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode shows a high <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 297&nbsp;Ω, and the resistance rises to 302&nbsp;Ω in the SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, which is due to the incorporation of the insulating SiO<sub>2</sub>. In contrast, after the removal of SiO<sub>2</sub>, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode exhibits a much-decreased <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 225&nbsp;Ω, which can be explained by the increased oxygen vacancies. Furthermore, the inclined line in the low-frequency region can be used to estimate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity based on Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> ( for more details, see Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0031_R_d254262246e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J. Sustainable Energy Fuels">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relationship of Z’ versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> is depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), and the slope value (<i>σ</i>) was calculated by linear fitting the Z’ versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> plots. The <i>σ</i>-values for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes are calculated as 63.6, 42.7, and 26.1, respectively. Obviously, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode displays the highest ion transfer kinetics according to Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also applied to analyze the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity at different discharge/charge states (for more details, see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4b</a>, all three electrodes show a similar trend upon sodiation/desodiation, among which the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode displays the highest diffusion coefficient. The low ion transfer resistance of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C can be well explained by the macropores as well as the high specific surface area offer a shortened ion diffusion path.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/70911166-b512-4a53-88bd-d7fa3df69f65/smll202302831-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/70911166-b512-4a53-88bd-d7fa3df69f65/smll202302831-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e590928d-40b0-49eb-8eef-14197399b42e/smll202302831-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/70911166-b512-4a53-88bd-d7fa3df69f65/smll202302831-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Nyquist plots with inset showing the equivalent circuits, and b) sodium–ion diffusion coefficient at different discharge/charge states for the TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes. c) CV curves at various sweep rates and d) corresponding log(peak current)-log(sweep rate) plots for Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode. e) Pseudocapacitive contributions for the TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes at various sweep rates. f) Pseudocapacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution for the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode at 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The sodium storage behavior can be revealed by the equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup>b</sup>, in which <i>i</i> is peak current and <i>v</i> is sweep rate, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are fitting values.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0032_R_d254262246e1558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nanomicro Lett.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>b</i> value can be used to determine whether the storage behavior is diffusion-controlled (<i>b</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.5) or pseudocapacitive (<i>b</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1), which can be obtained by fitting the log(<i>i</i>)-log(<i>v</i>) plots.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0033_R_d254262246e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>i–v</i> relationship can be revealed by performing the stepwise CV measurements at different sweep rates, which all display similar trends, that is, the cathodic and anodic peaks gradually become prominent as the sweep rate increases, while the shape is well maintained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the pseudocapacitive behaviors in the three electrodes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4d</a> shows the corresponding log(<i>i</i>)–log(<i>v</i>) plot, arising from the CV curves at various sweep rates. The <i>b</i> values for the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode can be fitted as 0.92/0.99 for the cathodic/anodic peaks 1/2, suggesting the sodium storage is mainly pseudocapacitive. Furthermore, the pseudocapacitive contribution can be quantitively calculated by the following equation of <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be evaluated by fitting <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>-<i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> plots, thus the pseudocapacitive (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) contribution can be separated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0034_R_d254262246e1637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), all three electrodes show an upward trend in pseudocapacitive contribution as the sweep rate increases, and the pseudocapacitive contribution is in good accordance with the <i>b</i> values. It is noteworthy that the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode shows the highest capacitive contribution of 87.0% at 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0004">4f</a>), which well explains its best rate performance as well as long-term cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>To explore the role of Si-doping on the sodium storage properties, ex situ Ti 2p and Si 2p XPS spectra were recorded at different states of the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5a</a> compares the Ti 2p XPS spectra of the pristine and discharged Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode, in which two prominent peaks shift to lower binding energies of 457.7 and 463.5&nbsp;eV after discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, corresponding to the Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. This result is in good agreement with the CV analysis. However, the Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti still remains in its original location after discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5b</a>, which indicates that the Si dopants did not participate in the electrochemical reaction upon sodiation. Furthermore, the ex situ TEM analysis was also performed to investigate the morphology evolution after cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5c</a>, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C still maintains its tablet-like feature even after 600 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which confirms its highly stable structure. HRTEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5d</a>) taken at the edge of the tablet shows no obvious lattice fringes, suggesting its amorphization upon repeated sodiation/desodiation. The HAADF-STEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5e</a>) and corresponding elemental maps (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0005">5f</a>) reveal that there is no phase separation even after 600 cycles, further confirming its robust structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fecae070-788c-402b-8daf-476d7e511a6a/smll202302831-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fecae070-788c-402b-8daf-476d7e511a6a/smll202302831-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3812e57d-2bdf-4cee-b551-1fc7e64d3157/smll202302831-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fecae070-788c-402b-8daf-476d7e511a6a/smll202302831-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ a) Ti 2p and b) Si 2p XPS spectra for the pristine and discharged Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrodes. Ex situ c) TEM, d) HRTEM, e) HAADF-STEM images, and f) corresponding elemental maps for the desodiated Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode after 600 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To get more insight into the key role of heteroatom doping as well as oxygen vacancies, density functional theory (DFT) calculation was conducted to study the DOS&nbsp;of TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0006">6a,b</a>). The TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C with oxygen vacancy shows a large bandgap of 2.061&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0006">6c</a>), indicating an inferior conductivity. Upon the introduction of heteroatoms, the bandgap of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C quickly decreases to 0.910&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0006">6d</a>), indicating an enhanced electron transferability. Moreover, ultraviolet-visible (UV-vis) diffuse reflectance spectroscopy was applied to calculate the bandgap energy of the samples. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (0.78&nbsp;eV) shows a lower bandgap compared to that for TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (1.03&nbsp;eV). Note that this value may not match well with the calculated results due to the carbon counterpart not being considered in DFT calculations. Furthermore, the charge density difference analysis shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0006">6e,f</a> illustrates the strong electronic coupling around Si-doping site and oxygen vacancy in Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C, which would be in favor of adsorption of Na+. The sodiation energy barrier (Δ<i>E</i><sub>sodiation</sub>) was analyzed based on Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202302831-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302831-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0011_R_d254262246e1767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett.">11</a>, <a href="#smll202302831-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302831-bib-0017_R_d254262246e1767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202302831-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1a785d00-0faa-4c95-963b-e1f77208e8fe/smll202302831-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202302831:smll202302831-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202302831-math-0001.png">

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{{\Delta}}{E}_{{\rm{sodiation}}} = {{\Delta}}{E}_{{\rm{sodiated\ TiO}}_2} - {{\Delta}}{E}_{{\rm{TiO}}_2} - {{\Delta}}{E}_{{\rm{Na}}}\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa601985-b49e-412c-9e9c-9bc8fdc678a6/smll202302831-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa601985-b49e-412c-9e9c-9bc8fdc678a6/smll202302831-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4626cc7d-6284-4fac-8137-f5db14433c00/smll202302831-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa601985-b49e-412c-9e9c-9bc8fdc678a6/smll202302831-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Structural models, c,d) calculated DOSs, e,f) corresponding charge density distribution, and g,h) Na+ intercalation models for a,c,e,g) TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and b,d,f,h) Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0006">6g,h</a>, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C displays a much lower Δ<i>E</i><sub>sodiation</sub> value of −0.95&nbsp;eV compared to that of TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (−0.25&nbsp;eV), which is beneficial to improving the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby enhancing the electrochemical performance of the electrode.</p>
         
         <p>To validate the potential application of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C for SIBs, Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full-cell SIB was assembled using Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C as the anode and commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0007">7a</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0007">7b</a>, the commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> delivers a reversible capacity of 110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C shows a capacity of 333&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, thus, the mass ratio of cathode to anode was set as 3:1 to balance the charge. The <i>d</i><sub>Q</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>V</sub> curves are also provided in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0007">7b</a> to determine the working voltage window of the full cell. The commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> shows obvious plateaus at 3.25 and 3.4&nbsp;V, while the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C delivers its main capacity below 2.2&nbsp;V, thus the operating window of the SIB was set as 1–3.4 V. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0007">7c</a>, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell SIB delivers a charge/discharge capacity of 205/178&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, based on the mass of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C. The capacity shows a slight decrease in the first few cycles due to the repeated formation of SEI film and limited sodium ions provided by the cathode. The as-assembled Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full-cell SIB delivers a high reversible capacity of 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 150 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302831-fig-0007">7d</a>) with a capacity retention of 84%.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302831-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44c3d271-de46-4e5d-923a-a2c44075629f/smll202302831-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/44c3d271-de46-4e5d-923a-a2c44075629f/smll202302831-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/35e62759-a1ed-4575-a8cb-4b7ca6fefb4f/smll202302831-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44c3d271-de46-4e5d-923a-a2c44075629f/smll202302831-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302831-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302831" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the configuration of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full-cell SIB. b) GCD profiles of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with inset showing the <i>d</i><sub>Q</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>V-V</sub> relationship. c) GCD profiles and d) cycling performance of Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell SIB at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302831-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302831-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we demonstrated the successful Si-doping into the MIL-125 MOF structure through a facile solvothermal, and its use of conformal precursor to fabricate SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C through annealing under an inert atmosphere, in which Si species existed as unbonded SiO<sub>2</sub> and lattice Si-doping. After NaOH etching, the removal of SiO<sub>2</sub> produced abundant inner pores, and the lattice Si-doped TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C) nanotablets with rich Ti<sup>3+</sup>/oxygen vacancies as well as abundant macropores were prepared, which efficiently enhanced the ionic/electric conductivity and sodium storage performance. When examined as an anode material for SIBs, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C demonstrated excellent electrochemical performance, delivering higher reversible capacity (285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and better rate/cycling performances (191.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 95.1%). Thanks to the novel hybrid structure with the combination of doped Si<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti heterostructure, carbon hybridization, abundant oxygen vacancies as well as the meso/macroporous structure, the Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C electrode exhibited improved reaction kinetics with enhanced ion/electron transfer rate and predominated pseudocapacitive sodium storage behavior (87% at 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). More importantly, this current synthetic strategy may shed light on developing advanced electrode materials with rational structures for high-performance energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302831-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302831-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302831-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302831-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302831-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302831-sec-0070-title"> Fabrication&nbsp;of Si-Doped MIL-125 (Si-MIL-125)</h4>
                  
                  <p>As for the fabrication of Si-MIL-125, 1.5&nbsp;g PTA (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Macklin) was dispersed in 27&nbsp;mL N,N-Dimethylformamide (DMF, C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>NO, Innochem) and 3&nbsp;mL methanol (CH<sub>4</sub>O, Innochem) with the help of ultrasonication. Then, 100&nbsp;µL TPOS (C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>28</sub>O<sub>4</sub>Si, Aladdin, 0.34&nbsp;mmol) and 780&nbsp;µL TBT, (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>36</sub>O<sub>4</sub>Ti, J&amp;K, 2.3&nbsp;mmol) were sequentially and quickly injected into the solution, which was then encapsulated in a Teflon vessel and kept at 165&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h under magnetic stirring (600&nbsp;rpm). The Si-MIL-125 crystals were obtained by centrifugation and washed with ethanol (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O, Innochem). For comparison, the pure MIL-125 was also synthesized without TPOS, while all other parameters were kept constant.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302831-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302831-sec-0080-title"> Synthesis of Si-Doped TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C (Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C)</h4>
                  
                  <p>Both TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C and SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C were obtained by annealing the as-prepared MIL-125 and Si-MIL-125 precursors under the argon atmosphere at 600&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h, respectively. The Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C was prepared by etching SiO<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOH aqueous solution under ultrasonic irradiation for 4&nbsp;h at 60&nbsp;°C, in order to remove the unbonded SiO<sub>2</sub> species (SiO<sub>2</sub> (s) + 2NaOH (aq) = Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> (aq) + H<sub>2</sub>O (l)). Afterward, the precipitate was rinsed with deionized water and diluted HCl alternatively several times until pH reached 7, followed by drying at 60&nbsp;°C overnight. Thus, the final Si-TiO<sub>2–x</sub>@C product was obtained.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
